   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Moons of Saturn
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:454px;max-width:454px">
        <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">
         Artist's concepts of the Saturnian ring–moon system
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:452px;max-width:452px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg">
           <img alt="A spherical yellow-brownish body (Saturn) can be seen on the left. It is viewed at an oblique angle with respect to its equatorial plane. Around Saturn there are rings and small ring moons. Further to the right large round moons are shown in order of their distance." data-file-height="970" data-file-width="2672" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg/450px-Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg/675px-Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg/900px-Saturn%27s_Rings_PIA03550.jpg 2x" width="450"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Saturn, its rings and major icy moons—from Mimas to Rhea
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:452px;max-width:452px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg">
           <img alt="In the foreground there are six round fully illuminated bodies and some small irregular objects. A large half-illuminated body is shown in the background with circular cloud bands around the partially darkened north pole visible." data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="7016" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg/450px-Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg/675px-Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg/900px-Moons_of_Saturn_2007.jpg 2x" width="450"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Images of several moons of Saturn. From left to right: Mimas, Enceladus, Tethys, Dione, Rhea; Titan in the background; Iapetus (top right) and irregularly shaped Hyperion (bottom right). Some small moons are also shown. All to scale.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        moons of
        <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
         Saturn
        </a>
       </b>
       are numerous and diverse,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151218-jc_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151218-jc-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ranging from tiny
       <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">
        moonlets
       </a>
       less than 1 kilometer across to the enormous
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , which is larger than the planet
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       has 62
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        moons
       </a>
       with confirmed orbits, 53 of which have names and only 13 of which have diameters larger than 50 kilometers, as well as dense
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        rings
       </a>
       with complex orbital motions of their own.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn62_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Saturn62-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep-main_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-shep-main-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009b_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009b-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Seven Saturnian moons are large enough to be ellipsoidal in shape, though only two of those,
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
        Rhea
       </a>
       , are currently in
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
        hydrostatic equilibrium
       </a>
       . Particularly notable among Saturn's moons are Titan, the second-
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">
        largest
       </a>
       moon (after Jupiter's
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       ) in the Solar System, with a
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan#Composition" title="Atmosphere of Titan">
        nitrogen-rich
       </a>
       Earth-like atmosphere and a landscape featuring dry river networks and
       <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">
        hydrocarbon lakes
       </a>
       found nowhere else in the solar system;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       since its chemical composition is similar to that of comets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20080326_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NS-20080326-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In particular, Enceladus emits jets of gas and dust which could indicate presence of liquid water under its south pole region and could potentially harbor a global ocean under its surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Twenty-four of Saturn's moons are
       <i>
        regular satellites
       </i>
       ; they have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">
        prograde orbits
       </a>
       not greatly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">
        inclined
       </a>
       to Saturn's equatorial plane.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They include the seven major satellites, four small moons that exist in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_moon" title="Trojan moon">
        trojan
       </a>
       orbit with larger moons, two mutually
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital_moon" title="Co-orbital moon">
        co-orbital moons
       </a>
       and two moons that act as
       <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_moon" title="Shepherd moon">
        shepherds
       </a>
       of Saturn's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F_Ring" title="F Ring">
        F Ring
       </a>
       . Two other known regular satellites orbit within gaps in Saturn's rings. The relatively large
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
        Hyperion
       </a>
       is locked in a
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        resonance
       </a>
       with Titan. The remaining regular moons orbit near the outer edge of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Ring" title="A Ring">
        A Ring
       </a>
       , within
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#G_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        G Ring
       </a>
       and between the major moons
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       and Enceladus. The regular satellites are traditionally named after
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">
        Titans
       </a>
       and Titanesses or other figures associated with the mythological
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">
        Saturn
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The remaining 38, all small except one, are
       <i>
        irregular satellites
       </i>
       , whose orbits are much farther from Saturn, have high inclinations, and are mixed between prograde and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">
        retrograde
       </a>
       . These moons are probably captured
       <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">
        minor planets
       </a>
       , or debris from the breakup of such bodies after they were captured, creating
       <a href="/wiki/Collisional_family" title="Collisional family">
        collisional families
       </a>
       . The irregular satellites have been classified by their orbital characteristics into the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Inuit_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Inuit group of satellites">
        Inuit
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Norse_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Norse group of satellites">
        Norse
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Gallic_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Gallic group of satellites">
        Gallic
       </a>
       groups, and their names are chosen from the corresponding mythologies. The largest of the irregular moons is
       <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">
        Phoebe
       </a>
       , the ninth moon of Saturn, discovered at the end of the 19th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       The rings of Saturn are made up of objects ranging in size from microscopic to
       <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">
        moonlets
       </a>
       hundreds of meters across, each in its own orbit around Saturn.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito2002_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Esposito2002-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus a precise number of Saturnian moons cannot be given, because there is no objective boundary between the countless small anonymous objects that form Saturn's ring system and the larger objects that have been named as moons. Over 150 moonlets embedded in the rings have been detected by the disturbance they create in the surrounding ring material, though this is thought to be only a small sample of the total population of such objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">
         <a href="#List">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           4
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           List
          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">

         </ul>
        </li>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg">
         <img alt="A large bright circle in the center is surrounded by small circles." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="936" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg/220px-Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg/330px-Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg/440px-Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iapetus-2010Mar04.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Saturn (
         <a href="/wiki/Exposure_(photography)#Overexposure_and_underexposure" title="Exposure (photography)">
          overexposed
         </a>
         ) and the moons Iapetus, Titan, Dione, Hyperion, and Rhea viewed through a 12.5-inch telescope
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_observations">
        Early observations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early observations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Before the advent of
       <a href="/wiki/Astrophotography" title="Astrophotography">
        telescopic photography
       </a>
       , eight moons of Saturn were discovered by direct observation using
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">
        optical telescopes
       </a>
       . Saturn's largest moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , was discovered in 1655 by
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       using a 57-millimeter (2.2 in)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objective_lens" title="Objective lens">
        objective lens
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap050325_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ap050325-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on a
       <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">
        refracting telescope
       </a>
       of his own design.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baalke_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baalke-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">
        Tethys
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
        Dione
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
        Rhea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
        Iapetus
       </a>
       (the "
       <a href="/wiki/Sidera_Lodoicea" title="Sidera Lodoicea">
        Sidera Lodoicea
       </a>
       ") were discovered between 1671 and 1684 by
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">
        Giovanni Domenico Cassini
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helden1994_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helden1994-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       were discovered in 1789 by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">
        William Herschel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helden1994_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helden1994-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
        Hyperion
       </a>
       was discovered in 1848 by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Cranch_Bond" title="William Cranch Bond">
        W.C. Bond
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/George_Phillips_Bond" title="George Phillips Bond">
        G.P. Bond
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond1848_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bond1848-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">
        William Lassell
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell1848_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell1848-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_exposure_photography" title="Long exposure photography">
        long-exposure
       </a>
       photographic plates made possible the discovery of additional moons. The first to be discovered in this manner,
       <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">
        Phoebe
       </a>
       , was found in 1899 by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Pickering" title="William Henry Pickering">
        W.H. Pickering
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering1899_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pickering1899-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1966 the tenth satellite of Saturn was discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/Audouin_Dollfus" title="Audouin Dollfus">
        Audouin Dollfus
       </a>
       , when the rings were observed edge-on near an
       <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">
        equinox
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fountain1977_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fountain1977-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was later named
       <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">
        Janus
       </a>
       . A few years later it was realized that all observations of 1966 could only be explained if another satellite had been present and that it had an orbit similar to that of Janus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fountain1977_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fountain1977-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This object is now known as
       <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">
        Epimetheus
       </a>
       , the eleventh moon of Saturn. It shares the same orbit with Janus—the only known example of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital_moon" title="Co-orbital moon">
        co-orbitals
       </a>
       in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uralskaya1998_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uralskaya1998-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1980, three additional Saturnian moons were discovered from the ground and later confirmed by the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
         Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       probes. They are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_moon" title="Trojan moon">
        trojan moons
       </a>
       of Dione (
       <a href="/wiki/Helene_(moon)" title="Helene (moon)">
        Helene
       </a>
       ) and Tethys (
       <a href="/wiki/Telesto_(moon)" title="Telesto (moon)">
        Telesto
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Calypso_(moon)" title="Calypso (moon)">
        Calypso
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uralskaya1998_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uralskaya1998-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_by_spacecraft">
        Observations by spacecraft
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Observations by spacecraft">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg">
           <img alt="Circular complex rings of Saturn are seen at the low angle. The rings look like two grayish bands running parallel to each other from the left to right and connecting at the far right. Half illuminated Titan and Dione are visible slightly below the rings in the foreground. Two bright dots: one at the lower edge of rings and another above the rings can be seen. They are Prometheus and Telepso." data-file-height="877" data-file-width="962" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg/200px-Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg/300px-Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg/400px-Cassini_-_four_Saturn_Moons.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Four moons of Saturn can be seen on this image by the Cassini spacecraft: Huge Titan and Dione at the bottom, small Prometheus (under the rings) and tiny Telesto above center.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="721" data-file-width="1018" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg/200px-Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg/300px-Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg/400px-Saturnian_moons_PIA14573.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Five moons in another Cassini image: Rhea bisected in the far-right foreground, Mimas behind it, bright Enceladus above and beyond the rings, Pandora eclipsed by the F Ring, and Janus off to the left.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The study of the outer planets has since been revolutionized by the use of unmanned space probes. The arrival of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
         Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft at Saturn in 1980–1981 resulted in the discovery of three additional moons—
       <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(moon)" title="Atlas (moon)">
        Atlas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">
        Prometheus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pandora_(moon)" title="Pandora (moon)">
        Pandora
       </a>
       , bringing the total to 17.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uralskaya1998_18-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uralskaya1998-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, Epimetheus was confirmed as distinct from Janus. In 1990,
       <a href="/wiki/Pan_(moon)" title="Pan (moon)">
        Pan
       </a>
       was discovered in archival
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       images.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uralskaya1998_18-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uralskaya1998-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       mission,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151218-jc_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151218-jc-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which arrived at Saturn in the summer of 2004, initially discovered three small inner moons including
       <a href="/wiki/Methone_(moon)" title="Methone (moon)">
        Methone
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pallene_(moon)" title="Pallene (moon)">
        Pallene
       </a>
       between Mimas and Enceladus as well as the second trojan moon of Dione—
       <a href="/wiki/Polydeuces_(moon)" title="Polydeuces (moon)">
        Polydeuces
       </a>
       . It also observed three suspected but unconfirmed moons in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F_Ring" title="F Ring">
        F Ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <span class="nowrap">
        November 2004
       </span>
       Cassini scientists announced that the structure of
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Saturn's rings
       </a>
       indicates the presence of several more moons orbiting within the rings, although only one,
       <a href="/wiki/Daphnis_(moon)" title="Daphnis (moon)">
        Daphnis
       </a>
       , had been visually confirmed at the time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt2004_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Britt2004-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2007
       <a href="/wiki/Anthe_(moon)" title="Anthe (moon)">
        Anthe
       </a>
       was announced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2007_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2007-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2008 it was reported that
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       observations of a depletion of energetic electrons in Saturn's
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       near
       <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
        Rhea
       </a>
       might be the signature of a
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Rhea" title="Rings of Rhea">
        tenuous ring system
       </a>
       around Saturn's second largest moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <span class="nowrap">
        March 2009
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aegaeon_(moon)" title="Aegaeon (moon)">
        Aegaeon
       </a>
       , a moonlet within the G Ring, was announced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In July of the same year,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2009_S_1" title="S/2009 S 1">
        S/2009 S 1
       </a>
       , the first moonlet within the B Ring, was observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009b_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009b-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In April 2014, the possible beginning of a new
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        moon
       </a>
       , within the
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        A Ring
       </a>
       , was reported.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140414a_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140414a-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" title="File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg">
        related image
       </a>
       )
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_moons">
        Outer moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Outer moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1006" data-file-width="1006" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg/220px-Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg/330px-Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg/440px-Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quadruple_Saturn_moon_transit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Quadruple Saturn–moon transit captured by the
         <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
          Hubble Space Telescope
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Study of Saturn's moons has also been aided by advances in telescope instrumentation, primarily the introduction of digital
       <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">
        charge-coupled devices
       </a>
       which replaced photographic plates. For the entire 20th century, Phoebe stood alone among Saturn's known moons with its highly irregular orbit. Beginning in 2000, however, three dozen additional irregular moons have been discovered using ground-based telescopes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A survey starting in late 2000 and conducted using three medium-size telescopes found thirteen new moons orbiting Saturn at a great distance, in eccentric orbits, which are highly inclined to both the equator of Saturn and the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
        ecliptic
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are probably fragments of larger bodies captured by Saturn's gravitational pull.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2005, astronomers using the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea_Observatory" title="Mauna Kea Observatory">
        Mauna Kea Observatory
       </a>
       announced the discovery of twelve more small outer moons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2005_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2005-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2005_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2005-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in 2006, astronomers using the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subaru_(telescope)" title="Subaru (telescope)">
        Subaru 8.2 m telescope
       </a>
       reported the discovery of nine more irregular moons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC8727_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC8727-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <span class="nowrap">
        April 2007
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tarqeq" title="Tarqeq">
        Tarqeq
       </a>
       (S/2007 S 1) was announced and in May of the same year
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_2" title="S/2007 S 2">
        S/2007 S 2
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_3" title="S/2007 S 3">
        S/2007 S 3
       </a>
       were reported.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC8836_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC8836-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of the 62 known satellites of Saturn are considered
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_asteroid" title="Lost asteroid">
        lost
       </a>
       because they have not been observed since their discovery and hence their orbits are not well-known enough to pinpoint their current locations. Work has been done to recover many of them in surveys from 2009 onwards, but seven –
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_2" title="S/2007 S 2">
        S/2007 S 2
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_13" title="S/2004 S 13">
        S/2004 S 13
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2006_S_1" title="S/2006 S 1">
        S/2006 S 1
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_3" title="S/2007 S 3">
        S/2007 S 3
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_17" title="S/2004 S 17">
        S/2004 S 17
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_12" title="S/2004 S 12">
        S/2004 S 12
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_7" title="S/2004 S 7">
        S/2004 S 7
       </a>
       – still remain lost today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">
        Naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Naming_of_moons" title="Naming of moons">
        Naming of moons
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The modern names for Saturnian moons were suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">
        John Herschel
       </a>
       in 1847.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helden1994_13-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helden1994-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He proposed to name them after mythological figures associated with the Roman god of agriculture and harvest,
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">
        Saturn
       </a>
       (equated to the Greek
       <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">
        Cronus
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helden1994_13-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helden1994-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In particular, the then known seven satellites were named after
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">
        Titans
       </a>
       , Titanesses and
       <a href="/wiki/Giants_(Greek_mythology)" title="Giants (Greek mythology)">
        Giants
       </a>
       —brothers and sisters of Cronus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering1899_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pickering1899-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1848, Lassell proposed that the eighth satellite of Saturn be named Hyperion after another Titan.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell1848_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell1848-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When in the 20th century the names of Titans were exhausted, the moons were named after different characters of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mythology" title="Greco-Roman mythology">
        Greco-Roman mythology
       </a>
       or giants from other mythologies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All the irregular moons (except Phoebe) are named after
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_mythology" title="Inuit mythology">
        Inuit
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology" title="Celtic mythology">
        Gallic
       </a>
       gods and after
       <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">
        Norse
       </a>
       ice giants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       share
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Name_conflicts_of_solar_system_objects" title="Name conflicts of solar system objects">
        the same names
       </a>
       as moons of Saturn:
       <a href="/wiki/55_Pandora" title="55 Pandora">
        55 Pandora
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/106_Dione" title="106 Dione">
        106 Dione
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/577_Rhea" title="577 Rhea">
        577 Rhea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/1809_Prometheus" title="1809 Prometheus">
        1809 Prometheus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/1810_Epimetheus" title="1810 Epimetheus">
        1810 Epimetheus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/4450_Pan" title="4450 Pan">
        4450 Pan
       </a>
       . In addition, two more asteroids previously shared the names of Saturnian moons until spelling differences were made permanent by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       (IAU):
       <a href="/wiki/Calypso_(moon)" title="Calypso (moon)">
        Calypso
       </a>
       and asteroid
       <a href="/wiki/53_Kalypso" title="53 Kalypso">
        53 Kalypso
       </a>
       ; and
       <a href="/wiki/Helene_(moon)" title="Helene (moon)">
        Helene
       </a>
       and asteroid
       <a href="/wiki/101_Helena" title="101 Helena">
        101 Helena
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sizes">
        Sizes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sizes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png">
         <img alt="A pie chart" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="683" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png/220px-Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png/330px-Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png/440px-Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_Saturnian_moons.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The relative masses of Saturn's moons. Mimas, the rings, and the small moons are invisible at this scale.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Saturn's satellite system is very lopsided: one moon, Titan, comprises more than 96% of the mass in orbit around the planet. The six other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">
        planemo
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">
        ellipsoidal
       </a>
       ) moons constitute roughly 4% of the mass, and the remaining 55 small moons, together with the rings, comprise only 0.04%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [a]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable sortable" style="clear:left;">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_groups">
        Orbital groups
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Orbital groups">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although the boundaries may be somewhat vague, Saturn's moons can be divided into ten groups according to their orbital characteristics. Many of them, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pan_(moon)" title="Pan (moon)">
        Pan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Daphnis_(moon)" title="Daphnis (moon)">
        Daphnis
       </a>
       , orbit within Saturn's ring system and have orbital periods only slightly longer than the planet's rotation period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2007_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2007-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The innermost moons and most regular satellites all have mean
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
        orbital inclinations
       </a>
       ranging from less than a degree to about 1.5 degrees (except
       <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
        Iapetus
       </a>
       , which has an inclination of 7.57 degrees) and small
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardSat_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardSat-40">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the other hand, irregular satellites in the outermost regions of Saturn's moon system, in particular the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_group" title="Norse group">
        Norse group
       </a>
       , have orbital radii of millions of kilometers and orbital periods lasting several years. The moons of the Norse group also orbit in the opposite direction to Saturn's rotation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ring_moonlets">
        Ring moonlets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ring moonlets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Rings of Saturn
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1440" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg/280px-PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg/420px-PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg/560px-PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg 2x" width="280"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA08319_Daphnis_in_Keeler_Gap.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Daphnis in the Keeler gap
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During late July 2009, a
       <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">
        moonlet
       </a>
       was discovered in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B_Ring" title="B Ring">
        B Ring
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009b_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009b-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       480 km from the outer edge of the ring, by the shadow it cast. It is estimated to be 300 m in diameter. Unlike the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Ring" title="A Ring">
        A Ring
       </a>
       moonlets (see below), it does not induce a 'propeller' feature, probably due to the density of the B Ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009s1_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2009s1-41">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="496" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/150px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/225px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/300px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Possible beginning of a new moon of Saturn imaged on 15 April 2014
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg/200px-PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg/300px-PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg/400px-PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18352-SaturnRings%26Moons-Enceladus%26Rhea-20150924.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Saturn's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F_Ring" title="F Ring">
          F Ring
         </a>
         along with the moons,
         <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
          Enceladus
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
          Rhea
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2006, four tiny moonlets were found in
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       images of the A Ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2006_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2006-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Before this discovery only two larger moons had been known within gaps in the A Ring: Pan and Daphnis. These are large enough to clear continuous gaps in the ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2006_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2006-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In contrast, a moonlet is only massive enough to clear two small—about 10 km across—partial gaps in the immediate vicinity of the moonlet itself creating a structure shaped like an airplane
       <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">
        propeller
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sremcevic2007_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sremcevic2007-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The moonlets themselves are tiny, ranging from about 40 to 500 meters in diameter, and are too small to be seen directly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2007, the discovery of 150 more moonlets revealed that they (with the exception of two that have been seen outside the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encke_gap" title="Encke gap">
        Encke gap
       </a>
       ) are confined to three narrow bands in the A Ring between 126,750 and 132,000 km from Saturn's center. Each band is about a thousand kilometers wide, which is less than 1% the width of Saturn's rings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This region is relatively free from the disturbances caused by resonances with larger satellites,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although other areas of the A Ring without disturbances are apparently free of moonlets. The moonlets were probably formed from the breakup of a larger satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sremcevic2007_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sremcevic2007-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is estimated that the A Ring contains 7,000–8,000 propellers larger than 0.8 km in size and millions larger than 0.25 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Similar moonlets may reside in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F_Ring" title="F Ring">
        F Ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2008_10-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2008-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There, "jets" of material may be due to collisions, initiated by perturbations from the nearby small moon Prometheus, of these moonlets with the core of the F Ring. One of the largest F Ring moonlets may be the as-yet unconfirmed object
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_6" title="S/2004 S 6">
        S/2004 S 6
       </a>
       . The F Ring also contains transient "fans" which are thought to result from even smaller moonlets, about 1 km in diameter, orbiting near the F Ring core.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray2008_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Murray2008-44">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One of the recently discovered moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Aegaeon_(moon)" title="Aegaeon (moon)">
        Aegaeon
       </a>
       , resides within the bright arc of
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#G_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        G Ring
       </a>
       and is trapped in the 7:6 mean-motion
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        resonance
       </a>
       with Mimas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This means that it makes exactly seven revolutions around Saturn while Mimas makes exactly six. The moon is the largest among the population of bodies that are sources of dust in this ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedman2007_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hedman2007-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In April 2014, NASA scientists reported the possible beginning of a new
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        moon
       </a>
       , within the
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        A Ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140414a_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140414a-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ring_shepherds">
        Ring shepherds
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ring shepherds">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Rings of Saturn
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="800" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg/220px-PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg/330px-PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg/440px-PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21449-SaturnMoons-Atlas-Daphnis-Pan-20170628color.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_satellite" title="Shepherd satellite">
          Shepherd satellites
         </a>
         – Atlas, Daphnis and Pan (color).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_satellite" title="Shepherd satellite">
        Shepherd satellites
       </a>
       are small moons that orbit within, or just beyond, a planet's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
        ring system
       </a>
       . They have the effect of sculpting the rings: giving them sharp edges, and creating gaps between them. Saturn's shepherd moons are
       <a href="/wiki/Pan_(moon)" title="Pan (moon)">
        Pan
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#Encke_Gap" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Encke gap
       </a>
       ), Daphnis (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keeler_gap" title="Keeler gap">
        Keeler gap
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(moon)" title="Atlas (moon)">
        Atlas
       </a>
       (A Ring),
       <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">
        Prometheus
       </a>
       (F Ring) and
       <a href="/wiki/Pandora_(moon)" title="Pandora (moon)">
        Pandora
       </a>
       (F Ring).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC2009_23-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC2009-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These moons together with co-orbitals (see below) probably formed as a result of accretion of the friable ring material on preexisting denser cores. The cores with sizes from one-third to one-half the present day moons may be themselves collisional shards formed when a parental satellite of the rings disintegrated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2007_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2007-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Co-orbitals">
        Co-orbitals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Co-orbitals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital_moon" title="Co-orbital moon">
        Co-orbital moon
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">
        Janus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">
        Epimetheus
       </a>
       are called co-orbital moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uralskaya1998_18-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uralskaya1998-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are of roughly equal size, with Janus being slightly larger than Epimetheus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2007_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2007-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Janus and Epimetheus have orbits with only a few kilometers difference in semi-major axis, close enough that they would collide if they attempted to pass each other. Instead of colliding, however, their gravitational interaction causes them to swap orbits every four years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_large_moons">
        Inner large moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Inner large moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png">
         <img alt="A circular part of a grayish surface, which is intersected from the top-left to the bottom-right by four wide sinuous groves. Smaller and shorter grooves can be seen between them running either parallel to the large grooves or criss-crossing them. There is a rough terrain in the top-left corner." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1684" data-file-width="1684" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png/200px-Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png/300px-Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png/400px-Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_south_pole_SE15.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tiger stripes on Enceladus
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
        <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">
         <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
          Saturn's rings
         </a>
         and moons
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg/200px-PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg/300px-PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg/400px-PIA18283-SaturnRings-TethysHyperionPrometheus-20140714.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          Tethys,
          <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
           Hyperion
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">
           Prometheus
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg/200px-PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg/300px-PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg/400px-PIA18353-SaturnRingsMoons-JanusTethys-20151027.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          Tethys and
          <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">
           Janus
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg/200px-PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg/300px-PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg/400px-PIA18355-SaturnMoon-Tethys-20151123.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          Tethys and the rings of Saturn
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The innermost large moons of Saturn orbit within its tenuous
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        E Ring
       </a>
       , along with three smaller moons of the Alkyonides group.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
         Mimas
        </a>
        is the smallest and least massive of the inner round moons,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006_37-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        although its mass is sufficient to alter the orbit of
        <a href="/wiki/Methone_(moon)" title="Methone (moon)">
         Methone
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006_46-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006-46">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is noticeably ovoid-shaped, having been made shorter at the poles and longer at the equator (by about 20 km) by the effects of Saturn's gravity.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Mimas has a large
        <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
         impact crater
        </a>
        one-third its diameter,
        <a href="/wiki/Herschel_(Mimantean_crater)" title="Herschel (Mimantean crater)">
         Herschel
        </a>
        , situated on its leading hemisphere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Mimas has no known past or present geologic activity, and its surface is dominated by impact craters. The only tectonic features known are a few arcuate and linear
        <a href="/wiki/Trough_(geology)" title="Trough (geology)">
         troughs
        </a>
        , which probably formed when Mimas was shattered by the Herschel impact.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
         Enceladus
        </a>
        is one of the smallest of Saturn's moons that is spherical in shape—only Mimas is smaller
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        —yet is the only small Saturnian moon that is currently endogenously active, and the smallest known body in the Solar System that is geologically active today.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Its surface is morphologically diverse; it includes ancient heavily cratered terrain as well as younger smooth areas with few impact craters. Many plains on Enceladus are fractured and intersected by systems of
        <a href="/wiki/Lineament" title="Lineament">
         lineaments
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The area around its south pole was found by
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        to be unusually warm and cut by a system of fractures about 130 km long called "tiger stripes", some of which emit
        <a href="/wiki/Jet_(fluid)" title="Jet (fluid)">
         jets
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
         water vapor
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">
         dust
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        These jets form a large
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plume_(hydrodynamics)" title="Plume (hydrodynamics)">
         plume
        </a>
        off its south pole, which replenishes Saturn's E ring
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and serves as the main source of
        <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
         ions
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Saturn" title="Magnetosphere of Saturn">
         magnetosphere of Saturn
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pontius2006_50-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Pontius2006-50">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The gas and dust are released with a rate of more than 100 kg/s. Enceladus may have liquid water underneath the south-polar surface.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The source of the energy for this
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
         cryovolcanism
        </a>
        is thought to be a 2:1
        <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
         mean-motion resonance
        </a>
        with Dione.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The pure ice on the surface makes Enceladus one of the brightest known objects in the Solar System—its
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometrical_albedo" title="Geometrical albedo">
         geometrical albedo
        </a>
        is more than 140%.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_49-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">
         Tethys
        </a>
        is the third largest of Saturn's inner moons.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006_37-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Its most prominent features are a large (400 km diameter) impact crater named
        <a href="/wiki/Odysseus_(crater)" title="Odysseus (crater)">
         Odysseus
        </a>
        on its leading hemisphere and a vast canyon system named
        <a href="/wiki/Ithaca_Chasma" title="Ithaca Chasma">
         Ithaca Chasma
        </a>
        extending at least 270° around Tethys.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Ithaca Chasma is concentric with Odysseus, and these two features may be related. Tethys appears to have no current geological activity. A heavily cratered hilly terrain occupies the majority of its surface, while a smaller and smoother plains region lies on the hemisphere opposite to that of Odysseus.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The plains contain fewer craters and are apparently younger. A sharp boundary separates them from the cratered terrain. There is also a system of extensional troughs radiating away from Odysseus.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The density of Tethys (0.985 g/cm
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        ) is less than that of water, indicating that it is made mainly of water ice with only a small fraction of
        <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
         rock
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2010_36-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2010-36">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
         Dione
        </a>
        is the second-largest inner moon of Saturn. It has a higher density than the geologically dead Rhea, the largest inner moon, but lower than that of active Enceladus.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        While the majority of Dione's surface is heavily cratered old terrain, this moon is also covered with an extensive network of troughs and lineaments, indicating that in the past it had global
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">
         tectonic
        </a>
        activity.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2009_51-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2009-51">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The troughs and lineaments are especially prominent on the trailing hemisphere, where several intersecting sets of fractures form what is called "wispy terrain".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2009_51-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2009-51">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The cratered plains have a few large impact craters reaching 250 km in diameter.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Smooth plains with low impact-crater counts are present as well on a small fraction its surface.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2009b_52-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2009b-52">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        They were probably tectonically resurfaced relatively later in the geological history of Dione. At two locations within smooth plains strange landforms (depressions) resembling oblong impact craters have been identified, both of which lie at the centers of radiating networks of cracks and troughs;
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2009b_52-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2009b-52">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        these features may be cryovolcanic in origin. Dione may be geologically active even now, although on a scale much smaller than the cryovolcanism of Enceladus. This follows from Cassini magnetic measurements that show Dione is a net source of plasma in the magnetosphere of Saturn, much like Enceladus.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2009b_52-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2009b-52">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <span id="The_Alkyonides">
       </span>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alkyonides">
        Alkyonides
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Alkyonides">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methone_PIA14633.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="448" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Methone_PIA14633.jpg/220px-Methone_PIA14633.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Methone_PIA14633.jpg/330px-Methone_PIA14633.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Methone_PIA14633.jpg/440px-Methone_PIA14633.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methone_PIA14633.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Cassini
         </i>
         image of Methone's leading side taken on 20 May 2012
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Three small moons orbit between Mimas and Enceladus:
       <a href="/wiki/Methone_(moon)" title="Methone (moon)">
        Methone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Anthe_(moon)" title="Anthe (moon)">
        Anthe
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pallene_(moon)" title="Pallene (moon)">
        Pallene
       </a>
       . Named after the
       <a href="/wiki/Alkyonides" title="Alkyonides">
        Alkyonides
       </a>
       of Greek mythology, they are some of the smallest moons in the Saturn system. Anthe and Methone have very faint ring arcs along their orbits, whereas Pallene has a faint complete ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alkyonides_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Alkyonides-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of these three moons, only Methone has been photographed at close range, showing it to be egg-shaped with very few or no craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELakdawalla,_E.2012_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELakdawalla,_E.2012-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Template:Harvard_citation_documentation#Wikilink_to_citation_does_not_work" title="Template:Harvard citation documentation">
          <span title="Template:Harvard citation documentation#Wikilink to citation does not work">
           citation not found
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Trojan_moons">
        Trojan moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Trojan moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_moon" title="Trojan moon">
        Trojan moon
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Trojan moons are a unique feature only known from the Saturnian system. A trojan body orbits at either the leading L
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
       or trailing L
       <sub>
        5
       </sub>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagrange_point" title="Lagrange point">
        Lagrange point
       </a>
