       of a much larger object, such as a large moon or planet. Tethys has two trojan moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Telesto_(moon)" title="Telesto (moon)">
        Telesto
       </a>
       (leading) and
       <a href="/wiki/Calypso_(moon)" title="Calypso (moon)">
        Calypso
       </a>
       (trailing), and Dione also has two,
       <a href="/wiki/Helene_(moon)" title="Helene (moon)">
        Helene
       </a>
       (leading) and
       <a href="/wiki/Polydeuces_(moon)" title="Polydeuces (moon)">
        Polydeuces
       </a>
       (trailing).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Helene is by far the largest trojan moon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while Polydeuces is the smallest and has the most
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaos_(physics)" title="Chaos (physics)">
        chaotic
       </a>
       orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006_46-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spitale_Jacobson_et_al._2006-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These moons are coated with dusty material that has smoothened out their surfaces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_large_moons">
        Outer large moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Outer large moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       These moons all orbit beyond the E Ring. They are:
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA08148_(Rhea-Splat).jpg">
         <img alt="A spherical body is almost fully illuminated. Its grayish surface is covered by numerous circular craters. The terminator is located near the upper-right limb. A large crater can be seen near the limb in the upper-left part of the body. Another smaller bright crater can be seen in the center. It is surrounded by a large bright patch having the shape of a five-pointed star." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg/220px-PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg/330px-PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg/440px-PIA08148_%28Rhea-Splat%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA08148_(Rhea-Splat).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Inktomi_(crater)" title="Inktomi (crater)">
          Inktomi
         </a>
         or "The Splat", a relatively young crater with prominent butterfly-shaped
         <a href="/wiki/Ejecta_blanket" title="Ejecta blanket">
          ejecta
         </a>
         on Rhea's leading hemisphere
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
         Rhea
        </a>
        is the second-largest of Saturn's moons.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In 2005
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        detected a depletion of electrons in the plasma
        <a href="/wiki/Wake" title="Wake">
         wake
        </a>
        of Rhea, which forms when the co-rotating plasma of
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Saturn" title="Magnetosphere of Saturn">
         Saturn's magnetosphere
        </a>
        is absorbed by the moon.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_22-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The depletion was hypothesized to be caused by the presence of dust-sized particles concentrated in a few
        <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Rhea" title="Rings of Rhea">
         faint equatorial rings
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_22-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Such a ring system would make Rhea the only moon in the Solar System known to have rings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_22-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        However, subsequent targeted observations of the putative ring plane from several angles by
        <i>
         Cassini'
        </i>
        s
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens#Instruments" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         narrow-angle camera
        </a>
        turned up no evidence of the expected ring material, leaving the origin of the plasma observations unresolved.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiscareno2010_56-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Tiscareno2010-56">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Otherwise Rhea has rather a typical heavily cratered surface,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        with the exceptions of a few large Dione-type fractures (wispy terrain) on the trailing hemisphere
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2008_57-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2008-57">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and a very faint "line" of material at the equator that may have been deposited by material deorbiting from present or former rings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2009_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2009-58">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Rhea also has two very large impact basins on its anti-Saturnian hemisphere, which are about 400 and 500 km across.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2008_57-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2008-57">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The first,
        <a href="/wiki/Tirawa_(crater)" title="Tirawa (crater)">
         Tirawa
        </a>
        , is roughly comparable to the Odysseus basin on Tethys.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004_48-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-Moore_Schenk_et_al._2004-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        There is also a 48 km-diameter impact crater called
        <a href="/wiki/Inktomi_(crater)" title="Inktomi (crater)">
         Inktomi
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inktomi_59-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Inktomi-59">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
         <a href="#cite_note-61">
          [b]
         </a>
        </sup>
        at 112°W that is prominent because of an extended system of bright
        <a href="/wiki/Ray_system" title="Ray system">
         rays
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Splat_60-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Splat-60">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
        which may be one of the youngest craters on the inner moons of Saturn.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2008_57-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2008-57">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        No evidence of any endogenic activity has been discovered on the surface of Rhea.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner2008_57-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wagner2008-57">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="663" data-file-width="744" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg/225px-PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg/338px-PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg/450px-PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg 2x" width="225"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18322-SaturnMoons-3Crescents-TitanMimasRhea-20150325.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Three crescent moons of Saturn: Titan, Mimas and Rhea
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
         Titan
        </a>
        , at 5,150 km diameter, is the second largest moon in the Solar System and Saturn's largest.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006_37-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Out of all the large moons, Titan is the only one with a dense (surface pressure of 1.5
        <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">
         atm
        </a>
        ), cold atmosphere, primarily made of
        <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
         nitrogen
        </a>
        with a small fraction of
        <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
         methane
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005c_62-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005c-62">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The dense atmosphere frequently produces bright white
        <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">
         convective clouds
        </a>
        , especially over the south pole region.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005c_62-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005c-62">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        On June 6, 2013, scientists at the
        <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Andaluc%C3%ADa" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Andalucía">
         IAA-CSIC
        </a>
        reported the detection of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">
         polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons
        </a>
        in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">
         upper atmosphere
        </a>
        of Titan.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAA-20130606_63-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-IAA-20130606-63">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
        On June 23, 2014, NASA claimed to have strong evidence that
        <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
         nitrogen
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">
         atmosphere of Titan
        </a>
        came from materials in the
        <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
         Oort cloud
        </a>
        , associated with
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">
         comets
        </a>
        , and not from the materials that formed Saturn in earlier times.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201450623_64-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-NASA-201450623-64">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The surface of Titan, which is difficult to observe due to persistent atmospheric
        <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">
         haze
        </a>
        , shows only a few impact craters and is probably very young.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005c_62-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005c-62">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It contains a pattern of light and dark regions, flow channels and possibly cryovolcanos.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005c_62-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005c-62">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopes2007_65-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lopes2007-65">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Some dark regions are covered by
        <a href="/wiki/Dune#Longitudinal_(Seif)_and_transverse_dunes" title="Dune">
         longitudinal dune
        </a>
        fields shaped by tidal winds, where sand is made of frozen water or hydrocarbons.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorenz2006_66-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lorenz2006-66">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Titan is the only body in the Solar System beside Earth with bodies of liquid on its surface, in the form of
        <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">
         methane–ethane lakes
        </a>
        in Titan's north and south polar regions.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stofan2007_67-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Stofan2007-67">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The largest lake,
        <a href="/wiki/Kraken_Mare" title="Kraken Mare">
         Kraken Mare
        </a>
        , is larger than the
        <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">
         Caspian Sea
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrakenMare_68-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-KrakenMare-68">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Like Europa and Ganymede, it is believed that Titan has a subsurface ocean made of water mixed with
        <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
         ammonia
        </a>
        , which can erupt to the surface of the moon and lead to cryovolcanism.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopes2007_65-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lopes2007-65">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        On July 2, 2014, NASA reported the ocean inside Titan may be "as salty as the Earth's
        <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">
         Dead Sea
        </a>
        ".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140702_69-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140702-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICARUS-2014_70-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ICARUS-2014-70">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
         Hyperion
        </a>
        is Titan's nearest neighbor in the Saturn system. The two moons are locked in a 4:3
        <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
         mean-motion resonance
        </a>
        with each other, meaning that while Titan makes four revolutions around Saturn, Hyperion makes exactly three.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006_37-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Antreasian_et_al._2006-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        With an average diameter of about 270 km, Hyperion is smaller and lighter than Mimas.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007b_71-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007b-71">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It has an extremely irregular shape, and a very odd, tan-colored icy surface resembling a sponge, though its interior may be partially porous as well.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007b_71-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007b-71">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The average density of about 0.55 g/cm
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007b_71-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007b-71">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        indicates that the porosity exceeds 40% even assuming it has a purely icy composition. The surface of Hyperion is covered with numerous impact craters—those with diameters 2–10 km are especially abundant.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007b_71-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007b-71">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is the only moon besides the small
        <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">
         moons of Pluto
        </a>
        known to have a chaotic rotation, which means Hyperion has no well-defined poles or equator. While on short timescales the satellite approximately rotates around its long axis at a rate of 72–75° per day, on longer timescales its axis of rotation (spin vector) wanders chaotically across the sky.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007b_71-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007b-71">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This makes the rotational behavior of Hyperion essentially unpredictable.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1995_72-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas1995-72">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg">
         <img alt="A part of a spherical body illuminated from the above and behind. The convex limb runs from the lower-left to the upper-right corner. The black outer space is in the upper-left corner. The terminator is near the bottom. The surface of the body is covered with numerous craters. A large ridge runs in the center from the top to bottom." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="579" data-file-width="1024" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg/220px-Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg/330px-Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg/440px-Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iapetus_equatorial_ridge.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Equatorial ridge on Iapetus
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
         Iapetus
        </a>
        is the third-largest of Saturn's moons.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_47-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Orbiting the planet at
        <span class="nowrap">
         3.5 million
        </span>
        km, it is by far the most distant of Saturn's large moons, and also has the largest
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">
         orbital inclination
        </a>
        , at 15.47°.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_38-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-NASA-38">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Iapetus has long been known for its unusual two-toned surface; its leading hemisphere is pitch-black and its trailing hemisphere is almost as bright as fresh snow.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005b_73-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005b-73">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        images showed that the dark material is confined to a large near-equatorial area on the leading hemisphere called
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini_Regio" title="Cassini Regio">
         Cassini Regio
        </a>
        , which extends approximately from 40°N to 40°S.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005b_73-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005b-73">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The pole regions of Iapetus are as bright as its trailing hemisphere.
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        also discovered a 20 km tall equatorial ridge, which spans nearly the moon's entire equator.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005b_73-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005b-73">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Otherwise both dark and bright surfaces of Iapetus are old and heavily cratered. The images revealed at least four large impact basins with diameters from 380 to 550 km and numerous smaller impact craters.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005b_73-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005b-73">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        No evidence of any endogenic activity has been discovered.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005b_73-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005b-73">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        A clue to the origin of the dark material covering part of Iapetus's starkly
        <a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">
         dichromatic
        </a>
        surface may have been found in 2009, when NASA's
        <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">
         Spitzer Space Telescope
        </a>
        discovered a vast, nearly invisible disk around Saturn, just inside the orbit of the moon Phoebe—the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoebe_ring" title="Phoebe ring">
         Phoebe ring
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbiscer2009_74-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Verbiscer2009-74">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Scientists believe that the disk originates from dust and ice particles kicked up by impacts on Phoebe. Because the disk particles, like Phoebe itself, orbit in the opposite direction to Iapetus, Iapetus collides with them as they drift in the direction of Saturn, darkening its leading hemisphere slightly.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbiscer2009_74-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Verbiscer2009-74">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Once a difference in albedo, and hence in average temperature, was established between different regions of Iapetus, a
        <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">
         thermal runaway
        </a>
        process of water ice
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
         sublimation
        </a>
        from warmer regions and
        <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(phase_transition)" title="Deposition (phase transition)">
         deposition
        </a>
        of water vapor onto colder regions ensued. Iapetus's present two-toned appearance results from the contrast between the bright, primarily ice-coated areas and regions of dark lag, the residue left behind after the loss of surface ice.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denk_75-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Denk-75">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_76-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Spencer-76">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Irregular_moons">
        Irregular moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Irregular moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1640" data-file-width="2338" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png/280px-Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png/420px-Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png/560px-Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png 2x" width="280"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_irregular_satellites.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram illustrating the orbits of the irregular satellites of Saturn. The inclination and semi-major axis are represented on the Y and X-axis, respectively. The eccentricity of the orbits is shown by the segments extending from the
         <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">
          pericenter
         </a>
         to
         <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">
          apocenter
         </a>
         . The satellites with positive inclinations are
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">
          prograde
         </a>
         , those with negative are
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">
          retrograde
         </a>
         . The X-axis is labeled in km. The prograde Inuit and Gallic groups and the retrograde Norse group are identified.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">
        Irregular moons
       </a>
       are small satellites with large-radii, inclined, and frequently
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_direct_motion" title="Retrograde and direct motion">
        retrograde
       </a>
       orbits, believed to have been acquired by the parent planet through a capture process. They often occur as
       <a href="/wiki/Collisional_family" title="Collisional family">
        collisional families
       </a>
       or groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The precise size as well as albedo of the irregular moons are not known for sure because the moons are very small to be resolved by a telescope, although the latter is usually assumed to be quite low—around 6% (albedo of Phoebe) or less.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The irregulars generally have featureless visible and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_infrared" title="Near infrared">
        near infrared
       </a>
       spectra dominated by water absorption bands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are neutral or moderately red in color—similar to
       <a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">
        C-type
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/P-type_asteroid" title="P-type asteroid">
        P-type
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/D-type_asteroid" title="D-type asteroid">
        D-type asteroids
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though they are much less red than
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [c]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inuit_group">
        Inuit group
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Inuit group">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Inuit_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Inuit group of satellites">
        Saturn's Inuit group of satellites
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Inuit group includes five
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">
        prograde
       </a>
       outer moons that are similar enough in their distances from the planet (186–297 radii of Saturn), their orbital inclinations (45–50°) and their colors that they can be considered a group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The moons are
       <a href="/wiki/Ijiraq_(moon)" title="Ijiraq (moon)">
        Ijiraq
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kiviuq_(moon)" title="Kiviuq (moon)">
        Kiviuq
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paaliaq_(moon)" title="Paaliaq (moon)">
        Paaliaq
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siarnaq_(moon)" title="Siarnaq (moon)">
        Siarnaq
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarqeq_(moon)" title="Tarqeq (moon)">
        Tarqeq
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The largest among them is Siarnaq with an estimated size of about 40 km.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gallic_group">
        Gallic group
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Gallic group">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Gallic_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Gallic group of satellites">
        Saturn's Gallic group of satellites
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Gallic group are four prograde outer moons that are similar enough in their distance from the planet (207–302 radii of Saturn), their orbital inclination (35–40°) and their color that they can be considered a group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are
       <a href="/wiki/Albiorix_(moon)" title="Albiorix (moon)">
        Albiorix
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bebhionn_(moon)" title="Bebhionn (moon)">
        Bebhionn
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Erriapus" title="Erriapus">
        Erriapus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Tarvos_(moon)" title="Tarvos (moon)">
        Tarvos
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tarvos, as of 2009, is the most distant of Saturn's moons with a prograde orbit. The largest among these moons is Albiorix with an estimated size of about 32 km.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norse_group">
        Norse group
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Norse group">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="1020" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg/200px-PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg/300px-PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg/400px-PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18357-SaturnMoons-TethysEnceladusMimas-CassiniHuygens-20151203.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Saturn's rings and moons – Tethys, Enceladus and Mimas.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Norse_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Norse group of satellites">
        Saturn's Norse group of satellites
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Norse (or Phoebe) group consists of 29
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">
        retrograde
       </a>
       outer moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman2001_26-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gladman2001-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are
       <a href="/wiki/Aegir_(moon)" title="Aegir (moon)">
        Aegir
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bergelmir_(moon)" title="Bergelmir (moon)">
        Bergelmir
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bestla_(moon)" title="Bestla (moon)">
        Bestla
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Farbauti_(moon)" title="Farbauti (moon)">
        Farbauti
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fenrir_(moon)" title="Fenrir (moon)">
        Fenrir
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fornjot_(moon)" title="Fornjot (moon)">
        Fornjot
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Greip_(moon)" title="Greip (moon)">
        Greip
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hati_(moon)" title="Hati (moon)">
        Hati
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hyrrokkin_(moon)" title="Hyrrokkin (moon)">
        Hyrrokkin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jarnsaxa_(moon)" title="Jarnsaxa (moon)">
        Jarnsaxa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kari_(moon)" title="Kari (moon)">
        Kari
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Loge_(moon)" title="Loge (moon)">
        Loge
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mundilfari_(moon)" title="Mundilfari (moon)">
        Mundilfari
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Narvi_(moon)" title="Narvi (moon)">
        Narvi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">
        Phoebe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Skathi_(moon)" title="Skathi (moon)">
        Skathi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Skoll_(moon)" title="Skoll (moon)">
        Skoll
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Surtur_(moon)" title="Surtur (moon)">
        Surtur
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Suttungr_(moon)" title="Suttungr (moon)">
        Suttungr
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thrymr_(moon)" title="Thrymr (moon)">
        Thrymr
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ymir_(moon)" title="Ymir (moon)">
        Ymir
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_7" title="S/2004 S 7">
        S/2004 S 7
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_12" title="S/2004 S 12">
        S/2004 S 12
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_13" title="S/2004 S 13">
        S/2004 S 13
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_S_17" title="S/2004 S 17">
        S/2004 S 17
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2006_S_1" title="S/2006 S 1">
        S/2006 S 1
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2006_S_3" title="S/2006 S 3">
        S/2006 S 3
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_2" title="S/2007 S 2">
        S/2007 S 2
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/S/2007_S_3" title="S/2007 S 3">
        S/2007 S 3
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After Phoebe, Ymir is the largest of the known retrograde irregular moons, with an estimated diameter of only 18 km. The Norse group may itself consist of several smaller subgroups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2007_34-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grav2007-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">
         Phoebe
        </a>
        , at
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
          7005213000000000000♠
         </span>
         213
         <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
          ±
         </span>
         1.4 km
        </span>
        in diameter, is by far the largest of Saturn's irregular satellites.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It has a retrograde orbit and rotates on its axis every 9.3 hours.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giese2006_78-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giese2006-78">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Phoebe was the first moon of Saturn to be studied in detail by
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        , in
        <span class="nowrap">
         June 2004
        </span>
        ; during this encounter
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        was able to map nearly 90% of the moon's surface. Phoebe has a nearly spherical shape and a relatively high density of about 1.6 g/cm
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        images revealed a dark surface scarred by numerous impacts—there are about 130 craters with diameters exceeding 10 km. Spectroscopic measurement showed that the surface is made of water ice,
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
         carbon dioxide
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllosilicate" title="Phyllosilicate">
         phyllosilicates
        </a>
        , organics and possibly iron bearing minerals.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Phoebe is believed to be a captured
        <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
         centaur
        </a>
        that originated in the
        <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
         Kuiper belt
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_25-8">
         <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It also serves as a source of material for the largest known ring of Saturn, which darkens the leading hemisphere of Iapetus (see above).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbiscer2009_74-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Verbiscer2009-74">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List">
        List
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: List">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Confirmed_moons">
        Confirmed moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Confirmed moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div style="float:left;width:66.4%">
       The Saturnian moons are listed here by
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital period
       </a>
       (or semi-major axis), from shortest to longest. Moons massive enough for their surfaces to have
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">
        collapsed
       </a>
       into a
       <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">
        spheroid
       </a>
       are highlighted in bold, while the irregular moons are listed in red, orange and gray background.
      </div>
      <div style="float: right; width:33.2%;">
       <table class="wikitable" style="margin:0;">
        <tr>
         <th colspan="5" style="background:#eaeaea">
          Key
         </th>
        </tr>
        <tr style="text-align:center;">
         <td style="background:#eef; width:19.9%;">
          †
          <br/>
          Major icy moons
         </td>
         <td style="background:#ccf; width:19.9%;">
          ♠
          <br/>
          Titan
         </td>
         <td style="background:#f4c2c2; width:19.9%;">
          ‡
          <br/>
          Inuit group
         </td>
         <td style="background:#fdd5b1; width:19.9%;">
          ♦
          <br/>
          Gallic group
         </td>
         <td style="background:#d3d3d3; width:19.9%;">
          ♣
          <br/>
          Norse group
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unconfirmed_moons">
        Unconfirmed moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Unconfirmed moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The following objects (observed by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       ) have not been confirmed as solid bodies. It is not yet clear if these are real satellites or merely persistent clumps within the F Ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco2005_19-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco2005-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothetical_moons">
        Hypothetical moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Hypothetical moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Two moons were claimed to be discovered by different astronomers but never seen again. Both moons were said to orbit between
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
        Hyperion
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solarviews_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solarviews-87">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Chiron_(hypothetical_moon)" title="Chiron (hypothetical moon)">
         Chiron
        </a>
        which was supposedly sighted by
        <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Goldschmidt" title="Hermann Goldschmidt">
         Hermann Goldschmidt
        </a>
        in 1861, but never observed by anyone else.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solarviews_87-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Solarviews-87">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Themis_(hypothetical_moon)" title="Themis (hypothetical moon)">
         Themis
        </a>
        was allegedly discovered in 1905 by astronomer
        <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Pickering" title="William Henry Pickering">
         William Pickering
        </a>
        , but never seen again. Nevertheless, it was included in numerous
        <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">
         almanacs
        </a>
        and astronomy books until the 1960s.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solarviews_87-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Solarviews-87">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">
        Formation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Saturn&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Formation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       It is thought that the Saturnian system of Titan, mid-sized moons, and rings developed from a set-up closer to the
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       of Jupiter, though the details are unclear. It has been proposed either that a second Titan-sized moon broke up, producing the rings and inner mid-sized moons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or that two large moons fused to form Titan, with the collision scattering icy debris that formed the mid-sized moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On June 23, 2014, NASA claimed to have strong evidence that
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">
        atmosphere of Titan
       </a>
       came from materials in the
       <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
        Oort cloud
       </a>
       , associated with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">
        comets
       </a>
       , and not from the materials that formed Saturn in earlier times.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201450623_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-201450623-64">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Studies based on Enceladus's tidal-based geologic activity and the lack of evidence of extensive past resonances in Tethys, Dione, and Rhea's orbits suggest that the moons inward of Titan may be only 100 million years old.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">

       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

           <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21151108" rel="nofollow">
            21151108
           </a>

          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.atitle=Origin+of+Saturn%27s+rings+and+inner+moons+by+mass+removal+from+a+lost+Titan-sized+satellite&amp;rft.volume=468&amp;rft.issue=7326&amp;rft.pages=943-6&amp;rft.date=2010-12&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F21151108&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fnature09661&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2010Natur.468..943C&amp;rft.aulast=Canup&amp;rft.aufirst=R.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoons+of+Saturn">
